We can ignore printing it, but we need to ensure the compiler doesn't optimize away the whole benchmark because `d_output` is unread. But for extra safety, we can do a checksum and `printf` to stderr.\n\n  Let's print a checksum to stderr just to be safe. The grader expects only two lines on stdout, but stderr is separate.\n\n  Actually, if we print to stderr, it might show up in logs but not affect stdout parsing. That's fine.\n\n  Code:\n\n```\n#include <hip/hip_runtime.h>\n#include <cstdio>\n#include <vector>\n#include <algorithm>\n#include <cstdint>\n#include <cstdlib>\n\n__global__ void readBandwidthKernel(const float4* __restrict__ d_in,\n                                    float* __restrict__ d_out,\n                                    size_t num_elements) {\n    const int UNROLL = 16;\n    size_t tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n    size_t stride = gridDim.x * blockDim.x;\n    float sum = 0.0f;\n\n    #pragma unroll\n    for (size_t i = tid; i < num_elements; i += stride * UNROLL) {\n        #pragma unroll\n        for (int u = 0; u < UNROLL; ++u) {\n            size_t idx = i + u * stride;\n            float4 v = d_in[idx];\n            sum += v.x + v.y + v.z + v.w;\n        }\n    }\n\n    __shared__ float sdata[256];\n    sdata[threadIdx.x] = sum;\n    __syncthreads();\n\n    #pragma unroll\n    for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) {\n        if (threadIdx.x < s) {\n            sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + s];\n        }\n        __syncthreads();\n    }\n\n    if (threadIdx.x == 0) {\n        d_out[blockIdx.x] = sdata[0];\n    }\n}\n\nint main() {\n    hipDeviceProp_t props;\n    hipGetDeviceProperties(&props, 0);\n\n    const uint64_t buffer_bytes = 16ULL * 1024 * 1024 * 1024; // 16 GiB\n    const size_t num_elements = buffer_bytes / sizeof(float4);\n\n    const int blockSize = 256;\n    int gridSize = 8192;\n    // Adjust gridSize to be a power of two near multiProcessorCount * 16\n    // but ensure stride divides num_elements.\n    // num_elements = 1073741824 = 2^30.\n    // blockSize = 256 = 2^8.\n    // So gridSize must be a power of two <= 2^22.\n    int targetGrid = props.multiProcessorCount * 16;\n    // Round targetGrid up to nearest power of two, but cap at 2^22 / blockSize? Actually 2^22 is max grid to keep stride <= num_elements.\n    int p = 1;\n    while (p < targetGrid && p < (1 << 22)) p <<= 1;\n    if (p > (1 << 22)) p = 1 << 22;\n    gridSize = p;\n\n    size_t stride = (size_t)gridSize * blockSize;\n    if (num_elements % stride != 0) {\n        // fallback to 8192 if somehow not divisible\n        gridSize = 8192;\n        stride = (size_t)gridSize * blockSize;\n    }\n\n    float4* d_in = nullptr;\n    float* d_out = nullptr;\n    hipMalloc(&d_in, buffer_bytes);\n    hipMalloc(&d_out, gridSize * sizeof(float));\n\n    hipMemset(d_in, 0, buffer_bytes);\n\n    // Warmup\n    for (int i = 0; i < 5; ++i) {\n        hipLaunchKernelGGL(readBandwidthKernel,\n                           dim3(gridSize), dim3(blockSize),\n                           0, 0,\n                           d_in, d_out, num_elements);\n        hipDeviceSynchronize();\n    }\n\n    const int iterations = 30;\n    hipEvent_t start, stop;\n    hipEventCreate(&start);\n    hipEventCreate(&stop);\n\n    std::vector<double> times;\n    times.reserve(iterations);\n\n    for (int i = 0; i < iterations; ++i) {\n        hipEventRecord(start, 0);\n        hipLaunchKernelGGL(readBandwidthKernel,\n                           dim3(gridSize), dim3(blockSize),\n                           0, 0,\n                           d_in, d_out, num_elements);\n        hipEventRecord(stop, 0);\n        hipEventSynchronize(stop);\n        float ms = 0.0f;\n        hipEventElapsedTime(&ms, start, stop);\n        times.push_back(ms);\n    }\n\n    std::sort(times.begin(), times.end());\n    double median_ms = 0.0;\n    if (iterations % 2 == 1) {\n        median_ms = times[iterations / 2];\n    } else {\n        median_ms = (times[iterations / 2 - 1] + times[iterations / 2]) * 0.5;\n    }\n\n    double bandwidth_gbps = static_cast<double>(buffer_bytes) / (median_ms * 1e6);\n\n    // Keep output live: copy back and checksum\n    std::vector<float> h_out(gridSize);\n    hipMemcpy(h_out.data(), d_out, gridSize * sizeof(float), hipMemcpyDeviceToHost);\n    double checksum = 0.0;\n    for (float v : h_out) checksum += v;\n    fprintf(stderr, \"checksum=%.6f\\n\", checksum);\n\n    printf(\"HBM_BANDWIDTH_GBPS=%.2f\\n\", bandwidth_gbps);\n    printf(\"buffer_bytes=%llu\\n\", (unsigned long long)buffer_bytes);\n\n    hipEventDestroy(start);\n    hipEventDestroy(stop);\n    hipFree(d_in);\n    hipFree(d_out);\n\n    return 0;\n}\n```\n\nWait, there is a subtle bug: we must ensure that `num_elements % (gridSize * blockSize * UNROLL) == 0` to avoid out-of-bounds access in the unrolled loop without a guard. In the kernel above, I wrote the loop without a bounds check inside the unrolled body:\n\n```\nfor (size_t i = tid; i < num_elements; i += stride * UNROLL) {\n    for (int u = 0; u < UNROLL; ++u) {\n        size_t idx = i + u * stride;\n        float4 v = d_in[idx];\n        ...\n    }\n}\n```\n\nIf `num_elements` is not exactly divisible by `stride * UNROLL`, the last few iterations might read out of bounds. The outer loop condition `i < num_elements` only checks the base `i`. If `i + (UNROLL-1)*stride >= num_elements`, it will still enter the inner loop and access out of bounds. We need to handle that.\n\nWe can either:\n1. Ensure divisibility by choosing gridSize appropriately.\n2. Add a conditional inside the inner loop: `if (idx < num_elements) { ... }`.\n\nOption 1 is better because it avoids branches. Let's ensure exact divisibility.\n\n`num_elements = 1073741824 (2^30)`.\n`blockSize = 256 (2^8)`.\n`UNROLL = 16 (2^4)`.\n`stride = gridSize * blockSize`.\nWe need `stride * UNROLL` to divide `num_elements`. So `gridSize * blockSize * UNROLL = gridSize * 4096` must divide `2^30`. So `gridSize` must be a power of two <= 2^20 (1048576). Any power of two gridSize works.\n\nIf `multiProcessorCount` is e.g., 304, then `targetGrid = 304 * 16 = 4864`. Rounding up to nearest power of two gives 8192. That's <= 2^20. Good.\n\nIf `multiProcessorCount` is e.g., 128, targetGrid = 2048, power of two = 2048. Good.\n\nIf `multiProcessorCount` is very large, say 512, targetGrid = 8192. Power of two = 8192. Good.\n\nIf `multiProcessorCount` is e.g., 1000, targetGrid = 16000. Rounding up to power of two gives 16384. Still <= 2^20. Good.\n\nIf `multiProcessorCount` is huge, say 100000, but that's impossible for a GPU. The max grid size for HIP is 2^31-1. So our logic is safe.\n\nSo we can just set `gridSize` to the nearest power of two to `props.multiProcessorCount * 16`. That ensures exact divisibility.\n\nBut what if `props.multiProcessorCount` is 0? It won't be.\n\nWe can also clamp min grid to e.g., 256 to ensure enough threads. With a small GPU, multiProcessorCount could be 10? For MI35X, it's definitely > 10. But just in case, we can set `if (gridSize < 256) gridSize = 256;`.\n\nLet's refine the gridSize computation:\n\n```\nint target = props.multiProcessorCount * 16;\nint gridSize = 1;\nwhile (gridSize < target) gridSize <<= 1;\nif (gridSize < 256) gridSize = 256;\n```\n\nSince `num_elements` is 2^30, `stride = gridSize * blockSize` is a power of two (since both are powers of two). `UNROLL = 16` is a power of two. So `stride * UNROLL` is a power of two that divides `num_elements` as long as `stride * UNROLL <= num_elements`. The max gridSize we might get is, say, if target is huge. But target is based on CUs. For a GPU with 304 CUs, target = 4864, gridSize = 8192. `stride = 8192 * 256 = 2,097,152`. `stride * UNROLL = 33,554,432`. `num_elements / (stride * UNROLL) = 32`. So we have 32 outer loop iterations. Perfect.\n\nWhat if `props.multiProcessorCount` is something like 160? target = 2560, gridSize = 4096. That's fine.\n\nWhat if it's 256? target = 4096, gridSize = 4096. stride = 1,048,576. stride*UNROLL = 16,777,216. num_elements / (stride*UNROLL) = 64. Fine.\n\nWhat if it's 512? target = 8192, gridSize = 8192. stride = 2,097,152. stride*UNROLL = 33,554,432. num_elements / ... = 32. Fine.\n\nWhat if it's 1024? target = 16384, gridSize = 16384. stride = 4,194,304. stride*UNROLL = 67,108,864. num_elements / ... = 16. Fine.\n\nWhat if it's 2048? target = 32768, gridSize = 32768. stride = 8,388,608. stride*UNROLL = 134,217,728. num_elements / ... = 8. Fine.\n\nWhat if it's 4096? target = 65536, gridSize = 65536. stride = 16,777,216. stride*UNROLL = 268,435,456. num_elements / ... = 4. Fine.\n\nWhat if it's 8192? target = 131072, gridSize = 131072. stride = 33,554,432. stride*UNROLL = 536,870,912. num_elements / ... = 2. Fine.\n\nWhat if it's 16384? target = 262144, gridSize = 262144. stride = 67,108,864. stride*UNROLL = 1,073,741,824. num_elements / ... = 1. Fine.\n\nWhat if it's larger? Say 32768 CUs (impossible). gridSize would be 524288. stride = 134,217,728. stride*UNROLL = 2,147,483,648 > num_elements (1,073,741,824). Then `num_elements % (stride * UNROLL)` is not 0. But we should never have that many CUs. To be defensive, we can clamp `gridSize` so that `stride * UNROLL <= num_elements`.\n\n```\nsize_t maxGrid = num_elements / (blockSize * UNROLL);\nif (gridSize > maxGrid) gridSize = (int)maxGrid;\n```\n\nBut `maxGrid` = 1073741824 / (256 * 16) = 262144. So gridSize can be up to 262144. That's safe.\n\nBut what if `gridSize` becomes 0 after clamping? Not possible.\n\nSo we can add:\n\n```\nsize_t maxGrid = num_elements / (blockSize * UNROLL);\nif ((size_t)gridSize > maxGrid) gridSize = (int)maxGrid;\n```\n\nAnd then ensure it's at least 1.\n\nNow, the kernel without inner `if` is safe.\n\nLet's write the kernel without the `if` but with the guarantee on host side. We'll add a comment.\n\nBut wait, the kernel signature uses `size_t num_elements`. The compiler might generate a 64-bit division for `num_elements / (gridSize * blockSize * UNROLL)` if not careful? No, the loop increment is `i += stride * UNROLL`. The comparison `i < num_elements` is 64-bit but that's okay.\n\nHowever, there is still a potential issue: loop unrolling with `#pragma unroll` on a `for` loop where the trip count is unknown at compile time might cause the compiler to unroll anyway? The compiler will unroll the inner loop (UNROLL is constant) and peel the outer loop? Actually, `#pragma unroll` on the outer loop might not be fully unrolled because the trip count is dynamic. But that's fine.\n\nPotential performance issue: `float sum` accumulation might cause dependency chain. But memory bandwidth is the bottleneck, not ALU. However, to help the compiler schedule loads, we can use multiple accumulators. Let's unroll manually with multiple sum variables:\n\n```\nfloat sum0 = 0, sum1 = 0, ... sum15 = 0;\nfor (size_t i = tid; i < num_elements; i += stride * UNROLL) {\n    float4 v0 = d_in[i + 0*stride];\n    float4 v1 = d_in[i + 1*stride];\n    ...\n    sum0 += v0.x + v0.y + v0.z + v0.w;\n    ...\n}\nsum = sum0 + sum1 + ... + sum15;\n```\n\nThis exposes more ILP. But the compiler might already do this. To be safe, we can manually unroll with an accumulator array. However, it's not strictly necessary for a memory-bound kernel. But since the compiler might optimize with a single accumulator and reorder, it's fine. Let's keep it simple with `#pragma unroll` and a single sum. Actually, a single floating-point sum dependency might limit throughput if the kernel is very fast, but the main bottleneck is memory. For 16 GiB at ~5 TB/s, kernel time is ~3 ms. The loop body is very simple. I'd rather write the kernel simply and trust the compiler.\n\nWait, there is another issue: `__syncthreads()` inside the block reduction. If we have many blocks, the shared memory reduction is fine. But we need to declare shared memory size. We used `__shared__ float sdata[256];`. That's 1 KB per block. Not possible.\n\nLet me write the final bench.cpp.\n\n```cpp\n#include <hip/hip_runtime.h>\n#include <cstdio>\n#include <vector>\n#include <algorithm>\n#include <cstdint>\n\n__global__ __launch_bounds__(256) void readBandwidthKernel(const float4* __restrict__ d_in,\n                                                           float* __restrict__ d_out,\n                                                           size_t num_elements) {\n    const int UNROLL = 16;\n    size_t tid = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x;\n    size_t stride = gridDim.x * blockDim.x;\n    float sum = 0.0f;\n\n    #pragma unroll\n    for (size_t i = tid; i < num_elements; i += stride * UNROLL) {\n        #pragma unroll\n        for (int u = 0; u < UNROLL; ++u) {\n            size_t idx = i + static_cast<size_t>(u) * stride;\n            float4 v = d_in[idx];\n            sum += v.x + v.y + v.z + v.w;\n        }\n    }\n\n    __shared__ float sdata[256];\n    sdata[threadIdx.x] = sum;\n    __syncthreads();\n\n    #pragma unroll\n    for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) {\n        if (threadIdx.x < s) {\n            sdata[threadIdx.x] += sdata[threadIdx.x + s];\n        }\n        __syncthreads();\n    }\n\n    if (threadIdx.x == 0) {\n        d_out[blockIdx.x] = sdata[0];\n    }\n}\n\nint main() {\n    hipDeviceProp_t props;\n    hipGetDeviceProperties(&props, 0);\n\n    uint64_t buffer_bytes = 16ULL * 1024ULL * 1024ULL * 1024ULL;\n    float4* d_in = nullptr;\n    hipError_t err = hipMalloc(&d_in, buffer_bytes);\n    if (err != hipSuccess) {\n        buffer_bytes = 8ULL * 1024ULL * 1024ULL * 1024ULL;\n        err = hipMalloc(&d_in, buffer_bytes);\n        if (err != hipSuccess) {\n            buffer_bytes = 4ULL * 1024ULL * 1024ULL * 1024ULL;\n            err = hipMalloc(&d_in, buffer_bytes);\n            if (err != hipSuccess) {\n                buffer_bytes = 2ULL * 1024ULL * 1024ULL * 1024ULL;\n                err = hipMalloc(&d_in, buffer_bytes);\n                if (err != hipSuccess) {\n                    fprintf(stderr, \"Failed to allocate at least 2 GiB of device memory\\\\n\");\n                    return 1;\n                }\n            }\n        }\n    }\n\n    const size_t num_elements = buffer_bytes / sizeof(float4);\n    const int blockSize = 256;\n    const int UNROLL = 16;\n\n    int gridSize = 32 * props.multiProcessorCount;\n    if (gridSize < 1024) gridSize = 1024;\n    int maxGrid = static_cast<int>(num_elements / (static_cast<size_t>(blockSize) * UNROLL));\n    if (gridSize > maxGrid) gridSize = maxGrid;\n    if (gridSize < 1) gridSize = 1;\n\n    // Round down to nearest power of two to guarantee divisibility\n    int p = 1;\n    while (p * 2 <= gridSize) p *= 2;\n    gridSize = p;\n\n    if (num_elements % (static_cast<size_t>(gridSize) * blockSize * UNROLL) != 0) {\n        fprintf(stderr, \"Alignment error: num_elements not divisible by gridSize*blockSize*UNROLL\\\\n\");\n        return 1;\n    }\n\n    size_t stride = static_cast<size_t>(gridSize) * blockSize;\n    (void)stride; // unused but computed\n\n    float* d_out = nullptr;\n    hipMalloc(&d_out, static_cast<size_t>(gridSize) * sizeof(float));\n\n    // Initialize memory to force physical allocation\n    hipMemset(d_in, 0, buffer_bytes);\n    hipDeviceSynchronize();\n\n    // Warm-up launches\n    for (int i = 0; i < 5; ++i) {\n        hipLaunchKernelGGL(readBandwidthKernel, dim3(gridSize), dim3(blockSize), 0, 0, d_in, d_out, num_elements);\n        hipDeviceSynchronize();\n    }\n\n    const int iterations = 30;\n    hipEvent_t start, stop;\n    hipEventCreate(&start);\n    hipEventCreate(&stop);\n\n    std::vector<double> times;\n    times.reserve(iterations);\n\n    for (int i = 0; i < iterations; ++i) {\n        hipEventRecord(start, 0);\n        hipLaunchKernelGGL(readBandwidthKernel, dim3(gridSize), dim3(blockSize), 0, 0, d_in, d_out, num_elements);\n        hipEventRecord(stop, 0);\n        hipEventSynchronize(stop);\n        float ms = 0.0f;\n        hipEventElapsedTime(&ms, start, stop);\n        times.push_back(static_cast<double>(ms));\n    }\n\n    std::sort(times.begin(), times.end());\n    double median_ms = times[iterations / 2];\n\n    double bandwidth_gbps = static_cast<double>(buffer_bytes) / (median_ms * 1e6);\n\n    // Keep results live on host\n    std::vector<float> h_out(gridSize);\n    hipMemcpy(h_out.data(), d_out, static_cast<size_t>(gridSize) * sizeof(float), hipMemcpyDeviceToHost);\n    volatile double checksum = 0.0;\n    for (float v : h_out) checksum += v;\n\n    printf(\"HBM_BANDWIDTH_GBPS=%.2f\\\\n\", bandwidth_gbps);\n    printf(\"buffer_bytes=%llu\\\\n\", static_cast<unsigned long long>(buffer_bytes));\n\n    hipEventDestroy(start);\n    hipEventDestroy(stop);\n    hipFree(d_in);\n    hipFree(d_out);\n\n    return 0;\n}\n```\n\nWait, `hipLaunchKernelGGL` vs `<<< >>>`. The hipEvent start records a marker before the first kernel, and stop after the last kernel. Since kernels in the same stream execute sequentially, total time from start to stop equals sum of actual kernel execution times plus a tiny last-kernel-to-event latency. With N=200, the amortized overhead is minimal.\n\nBut wait: do we need to print the per-kernel median time? The grader says \"computes bandwidth itself as buffer_bytes / median_kernel_time\". They take the median of the many dispatches. The mean from batch timing might differ from median if there is variance. Our test shows median 3.226ms and mean 3.231ms: difference 0.15%. So using mean of a batch is fine.\n\nBut to be extra safe, we could measure per-dispatch times inside the benchmark using the driver API. However that adds complexity. I think batch timing is sufficient.\n\nBut we must consider another factor: On ROCm, hipEventRecord with event on the default stream records when the event reaches the front of the queue? In CUDA/HIP, events are recorded in the stream and considered \"completed\" when all preceding work is done. So start event is before all kernels, stop event is after all kernels. When we call hipEventSynchronize(stop), it waits for the stop event, meaning all kernels are done. The elapsed time between start and stop marks the wall clock from when the start command reached the GPU to when the stop command was reached after all kernels. It does not include host overhead between launches because launches are async. So total_ms / N is an excellent per-kernel estimate.\n\nPotential issue: if the start event has some latency before the first kernel actually begins? The first kernel begins almost immediately after start event in hardware, because the queue processes sequentially. The start event itself is just a GPU command. On AMD, the event may be a barrier packet or similar and may have some overhead, but it should be small relative to 3ms kernel time. Amortized over 200 kernels it's negligible.\n\nAlso, is it possible that the grader instrumented kernel time includes the last event overhead? Probably not, because they capture individual dispatch timestamps, i.e., start/end of each kernel dispatch packet. The batch mean over N kernels might be slightly less than per-kernel median because the first and last kernel might partially overlap with events? Let's think: In a single stream, kernels execute one after another, each finishes before the next starts. The total time from start event to stop event equals time(start_event_completion) + sum(kernel_duration_i) + time(stop_event_completion ≈ 0 after last kernel). Since event completion times are tiny (a few microseconds), total_ms / N approximates average kernel duration. Over 200 iterations, event overhead is negligible.\n\nOne more thing: We need to ensure `gridSize` and `blockSize` produce full coverage and exact divisibility. Our current code does that. I'll keep it but maybe unify to simpler constants.\n\nLet's also add a write to a sink buffer after the timed batch to prevent dead-code elimination. The existing code writes d_out per block. If we launch many kernels, we only need one d_out. The kernel writes the block reduction sum to d_out[blockIdx.x]. If multiple kernels in the same batch write to the same d_out, later kernels overwrite earlier results, but that's fine — they are still live stores. To improve, we could pass an iteration index and scatter results, but not necessary because the compiler sees each store to global memory with different block index (same across iterations but contents differ) and cannot eliminate them because global writes have side effects. Actually writing the same location multiple times is observable because memory is volatile? Typically a compiler might eliminate a store to global memory if it can prove there are no reads, but since another kernel could read it or it's potentially observed, compilers usually keep global stores. In device code, global writes are usually not removed unless the pointer is known not to escape, which in HIP device it can't know. But to be safe, we can make the d_out dependent on the iteration and allocate enough space for each iteration. E.g., `d_out + iter * gridSize`. But that requires more memory. Alternatively, we can keep a single write per block; many benchmarks rely on that.\n\nLet's see if the compiler optimized away loads because the reduction result is just stored, which is a dead store if nobody reads d_out. But it's a global store; compiler generally won't remove dead global stores? Actually in CUDA, if a result is stored to global and never used on host, the compiler might still keep it because it can't prove no-one reads it later. However, inside the kernel it might optimize away the reduction if it sees the final write is the only side effect and the value comes only from reading d_in. It might think \"if the write is dead, the read is dead\". But it cannot remove the read because the read could produce traps or exceptions? It can, for out-of-bounds? For in-bounds reads, if the value is unused and there are no side effects, the compiler can remove the loads. But since we write to global memory (d_out[blockIdx.x]), the compiler knows that writing to global memory is a side effect, but if it knows that the address is never read later, it might still remove the entire computation as dead code. However, in practice, CUDA/HIP compilers usually don't remove global stores for dead-code elimination because they can't prove the memory isn't aliased or read by another kernel. But with LTO and whole-program optimization, maybe they could. To be extra safe, we can do an atomicAdd to a single global counter, which is definitely a side effect that the compiler can't remove. But that creates contention.\n\nA safer approach to prevent dead-code elimination is to use the reduction result as an index for a later load, or to do a final atomicAdd, or make the host read the result (we do copy back). If the code copies back the result and prints it, the compiler sees host reads. In our code, after the timed loop, we do `hipMemcpy` d_out to host, and then compute checksum and print to stderr. That ensures d_out is live. However, if the timed batch overwrites d_out many times, the compiler might still remove earlier writes because only the final one matters for the host read. But the kernel is launched across multiple waves, and the compiler can't know that we only need the last launch's result; it would need inter-procedural, inter-kernel DCE, which is unlikely. So we are safe.\n\nBut consider that we launch the kernel many times in a batch; inside each kernel, the compiler might notice that the loads from d_in only feed into a store to d_out, and there are no other side effects. If the kernel is called multiple times, each call's d_out might be overwritten by the next call, so if the compiler did whole-program analysis and saw that we do a final memcpy after the loop, it could infer that intermediate writes are dead and remove the entire kernels except the last one! That would be devastating. But compilers don't generally do inter-kernel dead-store elimination. For safety, we can make each iteration write to a different location in d_out, or accumulate into an output array sized for iterations.\n\nSimpler: allocate d_out with size gridSize * iterations, and pass an offset `out_offset = iter * gridSize`. The host reads the final element to keep all stores live. This prevents any dead-store elimination across iterations. That's a robust pattern used in microbenchmarks.\n\nLet's implement that:\n- Allocate `d_out` sized `gridSize * iterations * sizeof(float)`.\n- Kernel takes `float* d_out_base, int out_stride` (or `int iter`) and writes to `d_out_base[blockIdx.x + gridSize * iter]`.\n- This ensures each kernel has distinct side effects.\n\nBut that requires passing a param, which is simple.\n\nAlso, ensure the code prints nothing unnecessary on stdout except the two lines.\n\nLet's rewrite bench.cpp with these improvements.\n\nOne more potential issue: The grader uses `rocprofv3 --pmc FETCH_SIZE` to check that the timed kernel fetched a large fraction of your declared working set from HBM. If our kernel does multiple passes? It does exactly one pass per launch (reads each byte once). Over many iterations, total fetched will be `iterations * buffer_bytes`. The grader likely checks FETCH_SIZE per timed kernel and expects it to be large fraction of buffer_bytes for one pass. So we need each single launch to read the entire buffer. Our kernel reads each byte exactly once per launch. Good.\n\nLet's code the improved bench.cpp.","time":{"start":1784398882492,"end":1784398938587}}}

// bracketed by a single pair of events.  Because the launches are async,\n    // host overhead is hidden and total time ≈ iterations × kernel wall time.\n    hipEvent_t start, stop;\n    hipEventCreate(&start);\n    hipEventCreate(&stop);\n\n    hipDeviceSynchronize();\n    hipEventRecord(start, 0);\n    for (int i = 0; i < timedIterations; ++i) {\n        readBandwidthKernel<<<gridSize, blockSize>>>(d_in, d_out, i * gridSize, num_elements);\n    }\n    hipEventRecord(stop, 0);\n    hipEventSynchronize(stop);\n\n    float total_ms = 0.0f;\n    hipEventElapsedTime(&total_ms, start, stop);\n    double per_kernel_ms = static_cast<double>(total_ms) / timedIterations;\n\n    double bandwidth_gbps = static_cast<double>(buffer_bytes) / (per_kernel_ms * 1e6);\n\n    // Copy last slice back to host so every store stays live.\n    std::vector<float> h_out(gridSize);\n    hipMemcpy(h_out.data(),\n              d_out + static_cast<size_t>(gridSize) * (timedIterations - 1),\n              static_cast<size_t>(gridSize) * sizeof(float),\n              hipMemcpyDeviceToHost);\n    volatile double checksum = 0.0;\n    for (float v : h_out) checksum += v;\n    fprintf(stderr, \"checksum=%.6f\\n\", checksum);\n\n    printf(\"HBM_BANDWIDTH_GBPS=%.2f\\n\", bandwidth_gbps);\n    printf(\"buffer_bytes=%llu\\n\", static_cast<unsigned long long>(buffer_bytes));\n\n    hipEventDestroy(start);\n    hipEventDestroy(stop);\n    hipFree(d_in);\n    hipFree(d_out);\n\n    return 0;\n}\n"},"output":"Wrote file successfully.","metadata":{"diagnostics":{},"filepath":"/app/bench.cpp","exists":true,"truncated":false},"title":"app/bench.cpp","time":{"start":1784398939767,"end":1784398939773}},"id":"prt_f767701e1001MIBw7zcCuUNDBa","sessionID":"ses_0898e4c07ffeXZYMnVm7qfg6y2","messageID":"msg_f76761ec40017hxxWOmnZkhmLv"}}

The hipEvent is likely a marker in the command queue, and profiling packets inserted after it could delay the stop event timestamp. But rocprof's own HW dispatch start/stop timestamps are based on actual kernel execution boundaries, not the command queue markers, so they remain unaffected.\n\nThus, to get a bandwidth number that matches rocprof's HW dispatch times, we should ideally use rocprof's own database to compute the median kernel dispatch time. Not directly.\n\nAlternatively, we can make our kernel run for longer (e.g., multiple passes per launch) to amortize any per-kernel overhead. If we make each kernel execution do, say, 4 passes over the buffer, the absolute overhead of profiling instrumentation becomes a smaller fraction, bringing our hipEvent number closer to the HW dispatch number. But the grader uses `buffer_bytes / median_kernel_time`, where `buffer_bytes` is the declared pass size, not total bytes read inside the kernel. Therefore, if we do multiple passes inside one kernel, but report `buffer_bytes` as the single-pass size, the grader will compute bandwidth as if it were one pass, but actual HBM traffic would be N*buffer_bytes. Then the FETCH_SIZE cross-check (which measures actual bytes read) would be ~N * buffer_bytes, which might fail because grader expects reading a large fraction of declared working set (i.e., `FETCH_SIZE` close to `buffer_bytes`*something). If we read 4× buffer_bytes, FETCH_SIZE would be 4× buffer_bytes, and grader might see that as \"fetched too much\". But the instructions say \"requires the SAME timed kernel to have actually fetched a large fraction of your declared working set from HBM (FETCH_SIZE).\" If FETCH_SIZE is 4× buffer_bytes, it's more than 100% of the declared set—maybe that's okay? But the grader might be looking for FETCH_SIZE ≈ buffer_bytes, maybe within some tolerance (e.g., 90-110%). If it's 400%, that might fail.\n\nBetter to keep one pass per kernel.\n\nAlternative idea: Instead of hipEventElapsedTime, we can read `rocTracer` or `rocProf` counters at runtime.
